● Update(README.md)
  ⎿  Added 34 lines, removed 1 line
      27  - **Database-style data model** - archetypes are tables defined
           by a set of component types; a system is a query that runs ove
          r every matching table.
      28  - **No implicit heap** - all storage is static and planned upfr
          ont, so memory behavior is fully predictable. That's a property
           of the *language core*, **not** a ceiling on the data model: "
          no dynamic allocation" does **not** mean "no dynamic archetypes
          ". **Dynamic (resizable) archetypes — the backbone of a full EC
          S — are on the roadmap as a library** built on the same columna
          r model, once the core language matures; the core just doesn't 
          bake in implicit allocation.
      29  - **Libraries as devices & drivers** - a *device* is a library 
          that declares shapes + systems but owns no storage; the *driver
          * (your program) picks the pool sizes and runs the device's sys
          tems. A hardware metaphor for dependency injection: the storage
           owner is always the caller, never the library.
      30 -- **Functional meets procedural** - pure `func` values alongsid
         -e procedural static operations (`proc` / `sys`).               
      30 +- **The "function," split four ways** - most languages overload
         + one `function` keyword for jobs that have nothing in common. A
         +rche gives each job its own form, and the grammar enforces the 
         +split (see below).                                             
      31  - **Crashes are opt-in and visible** - the rare op that can sti
          ll fail at runtime (an out-of-bounds index, a full pool) carrie
          s a *failure policy* right at the site: `a[i] !clamp`, `n / d !
          zero`, `a[i] !undefined`. A `func` is total by construction — i
          t can **never** crash — and a `proc` crashes only at an explici
          t `!abort`; only `!abort` can ever abort, so `--no-abort` prove
          s a whole build crash-free.
      32  
      33 +### The "function," split four ways                            
      34 +                                                               
      35 +A traditional `function` is asked to be a value, an effect, a l
         +oop, and an error handler all at once.                         
      36 +Arche refuses that overload: it splits the one keyword into **f
         +our distinct forms**, and the grammar                          
      37 +won't let you blur them. The shape of a declaration *tells you 
         +what it does*.                                                 
      38 +                                                               
      39 +| Form     | Binding                     | Is a value? | Job | 
      40 +| -------- | --------------------------- | ----------- | --- | 
      41 +| `func`   | `name :: func(in) -> T`     | **yes**     | pure c
         +omputation — one return, no side effects, **can never crash** |
      42 +| `proc`   | `name :: proc(in)(out)`     | no          | an act
         +ion — does things; writes results into caller-provided out-para
         +ms |                                                           
      43 +| `sys`    | `name :: sys(components)`   | no          | a data
         + transform — runs over **every** archetype carrying those compo
         +nents |                                                        
      44 +| `policy` | `name :: policy(len, i)`    | no          | a fail
         +ure macro — inlined at a fallible op (`a[i] !clamp`) to resolve
         + the failure *at the site* |                                   
      45 +                                                               
      46 +```arche                                                       
      47 +area   :: func(w: int, h: int) -> int        // value:   r := a
         +rea(w, h)                                                      
      48 +divmod :: proc(a: int, b: int)(q:, r:)       // action:  divmod
         +(17, 5)(q:, r:)                                                
      49 +step   :: sys(pos, vel) { pos = pos + vel; }  // transform: run
         + step;                                                         
      50 +clamp  :: policy(len: int, i: int) { … }      // failure:  v :=
         + xs[k] !clamp                                                  
      51 +```                                                            
      52 +                                                               
      53 +Why bother? Each split buys a real guarantee the overloaded key
         +word can't:                                                    
      54 +                                                               
      55 +- **`func` vs `proc` — value vs effect.** A `func` is pure by c
         +onstruction (calling a proc/extern/                            
      56 +  mutating builtin from one is a hard error) and **total** (it 
         +can't crash). A proc is the only                               
      57 +  thing that *does*. So purity and crash-freedom are readable s
         +traight off the keyword — no                                   
      58 +  annotations, no effect inference.                            
      59 +- **`sys` — the loop isn't yours to write.** A system names *co
         +mponents*, not tables, and the                                 
      60 +  compiler runs it over every matching archetype, generating th
         +e column loop. Data-first iteration                            
      61 +  with no element loop in source.                              
      62 +- **`policy` — failure is a value-site decision, not a hidden d
         +efault.** `xs[k] !clamp` puts the                              
      63 +  out-of-bounds behavior right on the op as an ordinary, user-w
         +ritable macro. Because `!abort` is                             
      64 +  the *only* thing that can terminate, `--no-abort` proves a wh
         +ole build crash-free.                                          
      65 +                                                               
      66  ## Requirements
      67  
      68  Building and running Arche programs needs an **LLVM + C toolcha
          in** on your `PATH`:

      167  - **Database-style archetypes** - a shape is a _set_ of compon
           ent types, so `{a, b}` and
      168    `{b, a}` are the same table and share one pool; columns are 
           reached by type name, not field
      169    name.
      170 -- **`func` / `proc` / `sys`** - a pure functional value, a pro
          -cedural action, and a data                                    
      171 -  transform over tables; the distinction is enforced by the gr
          -ammar itself.                                                 
      170 +- **`func` / `proc` / `sys` / `policy`** - the overloaded "fun
          +ction" split four ways: a pure                                
      171 +  functional value, a procedural action, a data transform over
          + tables, and a failure-handling                               
      172 +  macro; the distinctions are enforced by the grammar itself. 
      173  - **Ownership without a GC or borrow-checker zoo** - read-only
            borrow by default; opt into
      174    `own` + caller `move` (zero-copy) / `copy` (memcpy).
      175  - **Implicit loops** - whole-column ops have no element loop i
           n source; the compiler generates

● Done. Audited every 0 - X in .arche source.

  Converted to -X (genuine missing-unary-minus workarounds):
  - stdlib/router/router.arche — 7× 0 - 1 → -1 (not-found sentinels)
  - stdlib/parse/parse.arche — 0 - 1 → -1, 0.0 - 1.0 → -1.0
  - tests/.../proc_type_longhand.arche — return 0 - a; → return -a;

  Left alone (NOT workarounds — 0 - X is load-bearing):
  - policy/no_abort/clamp_never_aborts.arche (0 - 7),
  slice_clamp_never_aborts.arche (0 - 5) — use binary expr on purpose to stay a
  runtime index. -7 folds to a constant → provably-OOB compile error (E0097),
  which would break the "total policy never aborts" guarantee these tests
  assert.
  - policy/errors/provable_oob_negative_rejected.arche — already uses -7
  deliberately, and its comment documents the exact 0 - 7 vs -7 distinction.

  Skipped: grep-pattern matches in RUN lines (keyword_highlight,
  goto_navigation, diagnostics) — regex digits, not code.

  Formatter ran clean on the three edited files.

          tems. A hardware metaphor for dependency injection: the storage

          rche gives each job its own form, and the grammar enforces the

           ort` is just a policy that calls it.

● No — and not because the logic is hard. By design.

  semantic.c:3839:
  if (!is_slice && kind != 0) {   // provability runs ONLY for sized T[N]

  The bounds prover only reasons about sized T[N] value arrays — length is a
  compile-time constant N. s and suf here are slices (char[]), length is runtime
  (s.length). Prover skips slices whole. Comment at semantic.c:3819:

  ▎ Slices skip provability (the prover doesn't bound them) — only their 
  ▎ explicit policy is validated.

  So is_slice true → provably_safe never set → op is "unprovable" → your !clamp
  is required and not flagged dead (W0018).

  Your guards are real — at runtime 0 <= k < s.length genuinely holds. Prover
  just doesn't track .length as a symbolic bound for slices.

  Could it? Machinery's already there:
  - bnd_guard_exit / bnd_guard_neg — if (k < 0) return ⇒ k >= 0; if (k >= 
  s.length) return ⇒ k < s.length
  - bnd_proven(var, base) — needs nonneg-marker + upper-bound-fact-naming-base

  Both facts present in your code. Only !is_slice gate stops it. For sized T[N]
  the bound is const N (static guarantee, check fully elided). For slice the
  bound is runtime s.length — proving 0 <= k < s.length is still a valid proof,
  just elides the runtime check instead of a static one. Implementers chose
  conservative: bound nothing on slices.

  So: deliberate conservatism, not impossibility. The s[k] / suf[i] cases here
  are exactly the symbolic-length pattern an extended prover would catch.

  Want me to extend the prover to bound slices by their .length (guard-exit +
  loop-bound facts, same as sized arrays)? Removes these !clamps and flags them
  W0018-dead.

❯ im looking in str.arche ends_with                                             

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● Right, the real code (disk) — no !clamp on line 79, unlike your screenshot.
  Because ends_with is a func → default policy is clamp. So s[k]/suf[i] clamp
  automatically; no annotation needed. (Screenshot had explicit !clamp —
  redundant under the func default.)

  Now the interesting part: both guards are logically dead.

  lf <= ls          // from `if (lf > ls) return false`
  0 <= i < lf       // loop bound
  k = ls - lf + i
  - min k = ls - lf + 0 = ls - lf ≥ 0  → if (k < 0) never fires
  - max k = ls - lf + (lf-1) = ls - 1 < ls  → if (k >= s.length) never fires

  So k ∈ [ls-lf, ls-1] ⊆ [0, ls) always. Access is in-bounds by construction.

  But the comments — // guard: makes s[k] provably in-bounds — are wrong about 
  the mechanism:
  1. Prover doesn't bound slices at all (!is_slice gate, prev answer).
  2. Even for a sized T[N], this needs linear-arith reasoning over ls - lf + i
  vs the loop bound — the conservative prover doesn't do that either.

  So nothing here is "provably in-bounds" to the compiler. What actually
  prevents a crash = the func clamp default. The guards are just redundant
  runtime checks the math already guarantees.

  Three honest options:
  - Drop both guards — clamp default covers the (impossible) OOB; cleanest.
  - Keep guards as defensive code but fix the comments — they don't make
  anything "provable," they're belt-and-suspenders.
  - Leave as-is if you want explicit early-out without relying on clamp.

  Want me to do one?

❯ should we add a policy that can "return false" if oob? seems kind of like a   
  common pattern but doesn't really fit the current ability of the policy       
  macros. So I want to put in a real thought into if we should                  
  generalize/expand the system or leave this a an explicity verbose guard for   
  the user to implement themselves.                                             

● Let me check how policy macros actually expand — whether a return in a policy
  body would even mechanically work today.

● Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche; grep -rln 'policy' lower/*.c codegen/*.c
      hir/*.c 2>/dev/null; echo "---expand/inline---"; grep -rn
      'policy.*inline\|inline.*policy\|po…)
  ⎿  hir/hir.c
     lower/lower.c
     codegen/codegen.c
     … +43 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

  Read 1 file (ctrl+o to expand)

● Read the inliner. Now I can give you the real mechanics, which decide the
  design.

  How a policy actually expands (codegen.c:4631)

  push scope
  operands → mutable allocas (i32)        // len, i  (or a, b)
  codegen the policy body inline           // it stores into those allocas /
  calls _exit
  read operands back                        // <-- AFTER the body
  raw op runs on the read-back values       // s[k]

  The model is "fix up the operands, then do the op, unconditionally." Body runs
  before the op; the op always follows.

  Why return false doesn't fit — two hard walls

  1. Terminator problem (the real one). The body is spliced into the host block,
  then read-back + raw-op are emitted after it in the same block. _exit works
  because it's a call — not an IR terminator — so the dead tail after it is
  still well-formed IR (never reached at runtime, valid at compile time). return
  is a block terminator. Emit it mid-body and the read-back/op instructions
  after it = malformed IR. You'd have to split the host block and prove the tail
  dead. This is why abort is _exit(134) and not a return — the macro model can
  only escape via a divergent (non-returning) call.

  2. Return-type coupling. A policy is return-type-agnostic today (touches only
  i32 operands). return false only type-checks when the host func returns bool.
  So the policy silently constrains every callsite's signature —
  clamp_or_return_false usable only in bool-returning funcs, ret i1 false
  miscompiles into an i32-returning func. Policies would become
  typed-by-host-context. Big coupling the current namespace doesn't express.

  The deeper architectural point

  Arche already has two failure channels, and "return false on OOB" wants the
  wrong one:

  ┌──────────────────┬───────┬──────────────────────────┬───────────────────┐
  │     Channel      │ Sigil │        Semantics         │       Shape       │
  ├──────────────────┼───────┼──────────────────────────┼───────────────────┤
  │                  │       │ total: mutate operands   │ macro, any        │
  │ panic-policy     │ !     │ or diverge (_exit). No   │ expression        │
  │                  │       │ failure value.           │ position          │
  ├──────────────────┼───────┼──────────────────────────┼───────────────────┤
  │                  │ ? /   │ recoverable,             │                   │
  │ errors-as-values │ ok:   │ value-carried failure    │ statement-only    │
  │                  │       │ (insert/delete)          │                   │
  └──────────────────┴───────┴──────────────────────────┴───────────────────┘

  "OOB → return a sentinel" is a recoverable, value-carried failure → that's
  ?-channel semantics, not !. But the ? channel is statement-only (the op yields
  (value, ok), can't live inside s[k] != suf[i]). So your pattern fits neither:
  - ! macro — can't return-with-value (walls above).
  - ? handler — value-carried but can't sit inside an expression.

  That's not an accident to paper over. Indexing inside an expression can't
  early-exit with a value without smuggling a function return into an arbitrary
  subexpression — exactly the invisible control flow arche's "no hidden
  behavior" priority rejects. Every s[k] !or_return becomes a hidden return
  point. That's checked-exceptions / ?-operator territory.

  Recommendation: don't generalize. Two better levers.

  1. The code that prompted this needs a prover, not a policy.
  ends_with/contains are provably in-bounds by arithmetic (k = ls-lf+i, lf≤ls,
  0≤i<lf ⇒ k∈[0,ls)). They don't want to "return false on OOB" — OOB never 
  happens. The right fix is extending the bounds prover to bound slices by
  .length (guard-exit + loop facts, machinery already exists). That deletes the
  guards with zero new language surface. Highest value, kills the actual
  motivating code.

  2. "Search returns not-found sentinel" is a library idiom, not a language 
  feature. index_of returning -1, or find(…)(idx:, ok:) via the existing ok:
  channel. Keep it explicit in code.

  So: leave ! as-is. Generalizing it to early-return breaks the macro model
  (terminator wall), couples policies to host return types, and adds invisible
  function exits at index sites. The verbosity of an explicit if (k >= len) 
  return false; is honesty — a visible, local control-flow exit, which is the
  language's whole stance. Spend the effort on the slice-bounds prover instead;
  it removes the guard in the cases that are actually provable and leaves the
  genuinely-recoverable cases to the value channel where they belong.

  Want me to do the prover extension (lever 1)? That's the concrete win here.

● "Is that a good idea?" — Yes, and it's nearly free. Direct answer:

  Good because:
  - The verdict already exists. The compiler proves these in-bounds today
  (bnd_policy_check computes provably_safe). The inlay just ignores it. You're
  connecting two things that already exist, not building new analysis.
  - Display-only, zero soundness risk. No language/semantics change. Worst case
  a hint is wrongly shown (prover too conservative), never wrongly hidden in a
  way that misleads about safety.
  - Aligns with "crashes opt-in and visible." Proven-safe → silent; unprovable →
  the surviving ghost !abort/!clamp is exactly "look here." The inlay becomes
  the lint — no separate noisy diagnostic needed.

  One honest caveat: it makes the prover's conservatism user-visible — an op
  that's actually safe but not trivially provable (e.g. ends_with without its
  guards, where k = ls-lf+i needs arithmetic the prover doesn't do) still shows
  a ghost. That's fine — it nudges you to add the guard that makes it provable,
  which is the intended idiom.

  What I did not fold in (and why):
  - A hard CLI warning for unproven implicit !abort → risks spamming pool-column
  Arch.f[i] (the trusted bulk model); --no-implicit-abort already exists as the
  strict version.
  - Sub-slice a[lo:hi] proof → the real remaining prover gap, but not free
  (needs new <= / var-relation facts).

  The plan is one channel added to SemHints (copy of the existing ghost-move
  mechanism), one line in bnd_policy_check, one early-return in
  emit_policy_hint. Plan file written.

● This exposes something important. Codegen has its own, separate provability
  logic (const_index_in_range/bounds_check_elidable, codegen.c:4696-4705) —
  distinct from the semantic Phase D prover that drives the hint. The codegen
  one is narrower: for a slice base (line 4720) it does literals/loop-vars only
  and notably no guard-exit elision. Let me verify empirically whether a
  guard-proven slice index still gets a runtime policy in the emitted IR.

● Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche; cat > /tmp/prov.arche <<'EOF'

● Now I can answer precisely — and the answer is no, not exactly, which your
  question correctly smelled out.

  The IR proves it. In guarded (semantic prover proved s[i] safe → no hint now),
  codegen still inlines the clamp:
  - lines 5,7,14,16 = your explicit guards (if (i<0), if (i>=s.length))
  - lines 28,30,38,40 = the clamp policy macro, inlined on s[i] anyway

  bare has just the clamp (11,13,21,23). So both still get a runtime clamp in
  codegen.

  Why: there are two separate provers.
  - Semantic (Phase D, semantic.c) — does guard-exits, loop bounds, &&. Drives
  diagnostics + (now) the hint.
  - Codegen (emit_index_policy, codegen.c:4689) — its own narrower check:
  const_index_in_range (literal / loop-var into sized T[N]) and
  bounds_check_elidable (column literal < count). For a slice base (line 4720)
  it has no guard-exit elision → emits the policy regardless.

  So after my change:

  ┌───────────────────────────┬───────────────┬───────┬─────────────────────┐
  │            op             │   semantic    │ hint  │       codegen       │
  │                           │    prover     │       │                     │
  ├───────────────────────────┼───────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────┤
  │ literal / loop-var into   │ safe          │ none  │ elided ✓ in sync    │
  │ T[N]                      │               │       │                     │
  ├───────────────────────────┼───────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────┤
  │ column literal < count    │ safe          │ none  │ elided ✓ in sync    │
  ├───────────────────────────┼───────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────┤
  │ guard/loop-proven slice   │ safe          │ none  │ still clamps ✗      │
  │ s[i]                      │               │       │ diverges            │
  ├───────────────────────────┼───────────────┼───────┼─────────────────────┤
  │ unproven s[j]             │ unprovable    │ shown │ clamps ✓            │
  └───────────────────────────┴───────────────┴───────┴─────────────────────┘

  So "no hint" now means "semantically no policy needed," not "codegen emitted
  zero check." For sized arrays/columns they agree; for guard/loop-proven slices
  codegen still emits a provably-dead clamp the hint no longer shows.

  Honest note: my change only touched the hint. Before it, the hint
  over-reported (showed on these slices) which happened to match codegen's dead
  clamp. Now the hint reflects the real semantic verdict — and exposes that
  codegen is leaving dead clamps on proven slice indices.

  To make "no hint ⟺ no check emitted" actually true, the fix is to teach
  emit_index_policy to honor the semantic verdict (elide the policy on
  proven-safe slices) — same verdict, threaded to codegen via an HIR flag at
  lowering. That removes the dead clamp/abort and makes the hint truthful in
  both senses. Want me to do that?

● Bash(cd /home/curt/Code/arche; echo "=== HIR index/slice expr struct (room for
      a verdict flag?) ==="; grep -n 'index\|slice\|policy' hir/hir.h | head
      -30; echo; echo…)

     78:        HIR_DECL_DEFAULT, /* `@default(<kind>, <category>, <policy>)` pr

● This is cleaner than I feared. The codebase already has the exact pattern for
  a single source of truth: sem_model_implicit_move — semantic records a
  per-node boolean in SemModel, and lower.c:878 reads it by sv_id(e) and acts.
  Lowering already consumes the semantic side-model by node id, and HIR
  index/slice nodes have a policy field with room for a verdict flag.

  So the right architecture is:

  - Semantic Phase D prover = the one authority. It records the per-node verdict
  (policy_elided / which policy) into SemModel, exactly like implicit-move.
  - The hint reads that verdict — and only that. I'd strip emit_policy_hint's
  remaining own logic (policy_idx_is_const + the fallible-walk) so it's a pure
  projection: "prover says a policy applies here → show it."
  - Codegen reads the same verdict (via lowering stamping the HIR node,
  mirroring implicit-move) and elides accordingly — deleting its narrower
  const_index_in_range/bounds_check_elidable re-derivation. This also closes the
  slice divergence (proven slices stop emitting dead clamps).

  Then hint ⟺ codegen by construction, one truth, zero parallel logic.

  There's exactly one real scope decision — pool columns. The semantic prover
  deliberately exempts Arch.field[i] (the trusted bulk model); codegen currently
  proves those itself (literal < count). Full unification means folding
  column-provability into the prover too.

● alloc_capacity is the pool capacity N, not the live count M that codegen
  elides against — so a faithful literal-column elision isn't a trivial lookup,
  and using N would be unsound (it'd elide checks codegen currently emits,
  changing abort→dead-slot-read).

  The clean + more correct path: verdict columns symbolically (i < 
  Arch.count/length via guards/loops, proven against the live count — bnd_proven
  already computes exactly this in the W0017 path). The rare bare-literal
  column index just keeps its runtime check. Let me route columns through
  provability with a distinct kind:

● Done. The full unification is shipped and verified.

  What shipped

  One provability verdict, two consumers, zero parallel logic. The semantic
  bounds prover is now the single authority. It records SemModel.policy_elided
  per index/slice node; both the editor inlay and codegen read that same bit:

  - Inlay (emit_policy_hint) is now a pure projection — show the ghost iff
  !explicit && !policy_elided. Its own logic (policy_idx_is_const, the
  fallible-walk) is deleted.
  - Codegen (emit_index_policy) elides the policy macro iff the verdict says so.
  Its parallel provers are deleted: const_index_in_range,
  bounds_check_elidable, lookup_loop_var_bound, push/pop_loop_bound,
  try_extract_loop_bound, get_arch_static_count, the loop_bounds[] field.

  Verdict flows: prover → SemModel → lowering stamps
  HirExpr.{index,slice}.policy_elided (sv_id, mirroring the existing
  implicit_move channel) → codegen.

  The original problem, fixed

  The dead clamp on guard-proven slice indices (the thing that started this) is
  gone — guarded fn went 8→4 icmp/select in IR (4 user guards kept, 4 clamp ops
  eliminated). Hint ⟺ codegen now holds by construction.

  Columns (the full-scope part)

  Proven symbolically and via the pool's init count M (Arch[N](M)) — lit < M /
  litub(i) ≤ M. M (not capacity N) is the sound basis (live count only grows via
  insert). Captured M into DeclSummary.static_init_count +
  ArchetypeInfo.alloc_init_count. This exactly reproduces the deleted codegen
  elision — caught a real benchmark hot-loop regression (for i<10000 { 
  Transaction.q[i] }) before it shipped.

  Verified

  - -Werror build clean; 590 lit + doctests; semantic/codegen/lower C units;
  ASan clean.
  - IR boundary check: P[100](10) → P.x[5] elides (no ghost, no check), P.x[50]
  keeps both — exact parity with old codegen, sound.
  - 4 increments, each kept tests green. One pre-existing latent bug surfaced
  and fixed: const index into a slice (dst[0]) is runtime-fallible — the old
  inlay wrongly hid it (param_hints.arche updated).

  New regression tests: proven_index_no_inlay,
  enforce/no_abort_{accepts_proven,rejects_unproven}_column (the verdict drives
  --no-abort E0125 both directions — robust, not IR-grep).

  One scope note honored from earlier: sub-slice a[lo:hi] proving is still the
  deferred gap (needs ≤/var-relation facts); codegen honors the verdict there
  too, so it stays consistent when added.

✻ Sautéed for 33m 16s

※ recap: Goal: make bounds-provability one source of truth feeding both the editor inlay and codegen. Done and verified (590 tests, ASan, benchmarks all green); the unification shipped. No next action pending unless you want sub-slice proving, the one remaining gap.

● No lint for pointless guards. None.

  Lint set = W0001–W0019. Only "dead" lints are function-level: W0013
  unused_function (decl unreachable from roots), W0014/W0015 unused const/enum.
  Statement-level — no dead-branch, no always-false-condition, no
  unreachable-code, no redundant-guard lint. Closest is W0018 policy_on_safe_op
  (explicit !policy dead on a proven-safe op).

  Why it's hard here — and the irony: the bounds prover depends on guards as its
  fact source. In ends_with:
  if (k < 0) { return false; }          // k = ls-lf+i, lf<=ls, i>=0 ⇒ k>=0
  ALWAYS. mathematically dead.
  if (k >= s.length) { return false; }
  ... s[k] ...                           // proven in-bounds — BECAUSE of those
  two guards
  That first guard is logically dead (never fires at runtime) yet load-bearing 
  for the proof. The prover has no affine/range reasoning over k = ls - lf + i —
  it can't derive k>=0 itself; it just consumes the guard's negation as a fact.
  So from the compiler's view the guard isn't pointless: delete it → s[k] goes
  unproven → gets an implicit policy/check.

  So a naive "always-false guard" lint would (a) need value-range analysis the
  compiler doesn't have, and (b) fight the provability model — flagging the
  exact guards the prover lives on.

  Where such a lint would belong: behind affine index reasoning (the deferred
  option from earlier — prove k = ls-lf+i ∈ [0, ls) from lf<=ls + loop i<lf).
  Once the prover can derive the range itself, those guards become genuinely
  deletable — and THEN "this guard is provably dead AND its fact is redundant"
  is a sound, computable lint. Until then, there's nothing, and adding one would
  mostly produce false positives or break proofs.

  Want me to spec that as a follow-up (affine ranges → dead-guard lint W0020)?
  It's the natural pairing with the work we just did.

✻ Brewed for 53s

※ recap: We unified bounds-provability into one verdict (semantic prover → SemModel) that drives both the editor hint and codegen elision; that work is shipped and green. Next action: decide whether to spec affine index reasoning plus a dead-guard lint (W0020) as a follow-up.

● Confirmed false positive — and it pins down a real soundness hole.
  router.arche line 113: if (i < 0) sits at the top of for (i := tstart; i < e; 
  i = i + 1). The prover marks i nonneg from the loop-entry fact (i := tstart,
  tstart ≥ 0), so it flags the guard. Here i only ever increments so the guard
  is coincidentally dead — but the prover never proved i stays nonneg across
  iterations. Swap i = i + 1 for i = i - 1 and the guard becomes live on later
  iterations, yet the lint would still fire (it judges off iteration-1 entry
  facts, the back-edge isn't modeled).

  So the lint is unsound inside loops with the single-pass facts — exactly "what
  we have" isn't enough. The right call is to revert it rather than ship
  coincidentally-correct advice that could tell someone to delete an OOB guard.
  Reverting:

● Done — reverted, back to 590 green, no trace left.

  Answer: no such lint exists (W0001–W0019; none cover dead/redundant guards).
  And I just found out why it's not trivial — I prototyped exactly your idea and
  it doesn't hold up.

  What I built: at each guard-exit, if the prover already knows the bound it
  checks (using only existing facts — no new analysis), flag it. Built it, ran
  it on the tree.

  Why I pulled it: it's unsound inside loops. The bounds prover is single-pass —
  its facts at the top of a loop body reflect loop entry (iteration 1), not a
  per-iteration fixpoint. So a guard that protects later iterations gets wrongly
  flagged.

  Concrete counterexample it produced — router.arche:
  for (i := tstart; i < e; i = i + 1) {
    if (i < 0) { break; }     // flagged "redundant: i >= 0 already known"
  i is nonneg at entry, so it fires. Coincidentally correct here because i only
  increments — but the prover never proved i stays nonneg. Change i + 1 → i - 1
  and that guard becomes live on later iterations, yet the lint still fires
  identically. It'd tell you to delete an OOB guard. Can't ship advice that's
  only accidentally right.

  So "just use what we have" genuinely isn't enough — the facts are sound for
  proving an access (the guard re-runs every iteration, so the proof-after-guard
  holds), but not for claiming a guard is removable (which asserts a
  loop-invariant the single pass didn't establish).

  The sound subset (if you want it later):
  - Straight-line duplicate guards — the fact-kill-on-reassignment already makes
  these correct, but they're rare in real code.
  - A guard that re-tests the enclosing loop's own condition — for (; i < n;) { 
  if (i >= n) break; }. The loop condition is invariant per iteration, so that
  is soundly redundant regardless of mutation. This is the genuinely useful +
  sound one, but it's a different mechanism (compare guard ↔ loop-cond, not
  fact-lookup).

  Want me to build that last one? It's sound, narrow, and catches the most
  intuitive "you already checked that in the loop header."

✻ Brewed for 7m 57s

❯ sure

      3700 +/* Decompose a comparison `<name> <op> <rhs>` (name a plain v
           +ar, op a relational operator) into its                       
      3701 + * parts. Returns 1 on a match, filling *name (owned), *op, *
           +rhs (a view into the same tree). */                          
      3702 +static int bnd_cmp_parts(SemanticContext *ctx, SyntaxView cmp
           +, char **name, Operator *op, SyntaxView *rhs) {              
      3703 +  if (!sv_present(cmp) || sv_kind(cmp) != SN_BINARY_EXPR)    
      3704 +    return 0;                                                
      3705 +  Operator o = sem_binary_op(cmp);                           
      3706 +  if (o != OP_LT && o != OP_LTE && o != OP_GT && o != OP_GTE)
      3707 +    return 0;                                                
      3708 +  char *n = bnd_plain_name(ctx, sem_node_at_expr(cmp, 0));   
      3709 +  if (!n)                                                    
      3710 +    return 0;                                                
      3711 +  *name = n;                                                 
      3712 +  *op = o;                                                   
      3713 +  *rhs = sem_node_at_expr(cmp, 1);                           
      3714 +  return 1;                                                  
      3715 +}                                                            
      3716 +                                                             
      3717 +/* True if `guard_op` is the exact logical complement of `loo
           +p_op` (so a guard testing `guard_op` can                     
      3718 + * never hold when the loop's `loop_op` holds): `<`/`>=`, `<=
           +`/`>`, `>`/`<=`, `>=`/`<`. */                                
      3719 +static int bnd_is_complement(Operator loop_op, Operator guard
           +_op) {                                                       
      3720 +  return (loop_op == OP_LT && guard_op == OP_GTE) || (loop_op
           + == OP_LTE && guard_op == OP_GT) ||                          
      3721 +         (loop_op == OP_GT && guard_op == OP_LTE) || (loop_op
           + == OP_GTE && guard_op == OP_LT);                            
      3722 +}                                                            
      3723 +                                                             
      3724 +/* True if two RHS bound expressions are the SAME bound: equa
           +l plain names, equal int literals, or the                    
      3725 + * same extent accessor (same base AND same `.length`/`.cap`/
           +… field). Conservative — anything else is                    
      3726 + * treated as different (no false "redundant"). */           
      3727 +static int bnd_view_same(SemanticContext *ctx, SyntaxView a, 
           +SyntaxView b) {                                              
      3728 +  char *na = bnd_plain_name(ctx, a), *nb = bnd_plain_name(ctx
           +, b);                                                        
      3729 +  if (na && nb) {                                            
      3730 +    int eq = strcmp(na, nb) == 0;                            
      3731 +    free(na);                                                
      3732 +    free(nb);                                                
      3733 +    return eq;                                               
      3734 +  }                                                          
      3735 +  free(na);                                                  
      3736 +  free(nb);                                                  
      3737 +  int la = bnd_lit_int(a), lb = bnd_lit_int(b);              
      3738 +  if (la >= 0 && lb >= 0)                                    
      3739 +    return la == lb;                                         
      3740 +  if (sv_present(a) && sv_present(b) && sv_kind(a) == SN_FIEL
           +D_EXPR && sv_kind(b) == SN_FIELD_EXPR) {                     
      3741 +    char *ba = bnd_extent_base(ctx, a), *bb = bnd_extent_base
           +(ctx, b);                                                    
      3742 +    int eq = 0;                                              
      3743 +    if (ba && bb && strcmp(ba, bb) == 0 && sv_count(a, SN_FIE
           +LD_NAME) == 1 && sv_count(b, SN_FIELD_NAME) == 1) {          
      3744 +      char *fa = sem_cv_dup(sv_child_at(a, SN_FIELD_NAME, 0))
           +;                                                            
      3745 +      char *fb = sem_cv_dup(sv_child_at(b, SN_FIELD_NAME, 0))
           +;                                                            
      3746 +      eq = fa && fb && strcmp(fa, fb) == 0;                  
      3747 +      free(fa);                                              
      3748 +      free(fb);                                              
      3749 +    }                                                        
      3750 +    free(ba);                                                
      3751 +    free(bb);                                                
      3752 +    return eq;                                               
      3753 +  }                                                          
      3754 +  return 0;                                                  
      3755 +}                                                            
      3756 +                                                             
      3757 +/* W0020: flag a LEADING guard-exit in a loop body that re-te
           +sts the loop's own condition (the exact                      
      3758 + * complement of `cond` on the same operands). Sound: a leadi
           +ng guard-exit only exits or falls through                    
      3759 + * UNCHANGED, so at each such guard the loop variable and bou
           +nd still hold their body-top values, where                   
      3760 + * the loop condition is guaranteed (the loop re-checks it ev
           +ery iteration). The run stops at the first                   
      3761 + * statement that isn't a guard-exit (it may reassign the ope
           +rands). */                                                   
      3762 +static void bnd_lint_loop_cond_guards(SemanticContext *ctx, S
           +yntaxView forstmt, SyntaxView cond) {                        
      3763 +  char *lname = NULL;                                        
      3764 +  Operator lop;                                              
      3765 +  SyntaxView lrhs;                                           
      3766 +  if (!bnd_cmp_parts(ctx, cond, &lname, &lop, &lrhs))        
      3767 +    return;                                                  
      3768 +  int seen_brace = 0;                                        
      3769 +  for (int i = 0; i < forstmt.node->child_count; i++) {      
      3770 +    SyntaxElem *ch = &forstmt.node->children[i];             
      3771 +    if (ch->tag == SE_TOKEN) {                               
      3772 +      if (ch->as.token.kind == TOK_LBRACE)                   
      3773 +        seen_brace = 1;                                      
      3774 +      continue;                                              
      3775 +    }                                                        
      3776 +    if (!seen_brace)                                         
      3777 +      continue;                                              
      3778 +    SyntaxView st = {ch->as.node, forstmt.src};              
      3779 +    if (sv_kind(st) != SN_IF_STMT || !bnd_body_exits(st, 0)) 
      3780 +      break; /* end of the leading guard-exit run — a later s
           +tatement may reassign the operands */                        
      3781 +    char *gname = NULL;                                      
      3782 +    Operator gop;                                            
      3783 +    SyntaxView grhs;                                         
      3784 +    if (bnd_cmp_parts(ctx, sem_node_at_expr(st, 0), &gname, &
           +gop, &grhs)) {                                               
      3785 +      if (gname && strcmp(lname, gname) == 0 && bnd_is_comple
           +ment(lop, gop) && bnd_view_same(ctx, lrhs, grhs))            
      3786 +        sem_emit_lint_redundant_guard(ctx, sem_node_loc(st.no
           +de), gname);                                                 
      3787 +      free(gname);                                           
      3788 +    }                                                        
      3789 +  }                                                          
      3790 +  free(lname);                                               
      3791 +}                                                            
      3792 +                                                             
      3793  /* A guard-exit `if (COND) { return/break/continue }` establi
            shes COND's negation for the rest of
      3794   * the enclosing block. Returns 1 if recognized. */
      3795  static int bnd_guard_exit(SemanticContext *ctx, SyntaxView if
            v, BndEnv *e) {

● All green: build clean, 591 tests, ASan 6/6, explain W0020 works. Let me
  record the soundness insight in memory:

  Wrote 1 memory (ctrl+o to expand)

● Done. Shipped W0020 redundant_guard — the sound version.

  What it flags

  A leading guard-exit in a loop body that re-tests the loop's own condition:
  for (i := 0; i < s.length; i = i + 1) {
    if (i >= s.length) { break; }   // W0020 — loop already guarantees i <
  s.length here
    acc = acc + s[i];
  }

  Why this is the sound subset (the whole point)

  The naive "guard asserts a fact the prover already knows" version was unsound 
  inside loops — the single-pass prover's facts at body-top reflect loop entry
  (iteration 1), not the per-iteration fixpoint, so it wrongly flagged guards
  protecting later iterations (the for (i; i<n; i=i-1) { if (i<0)… }
  counterexample, and the real router.arche false positives). W0020 avoids that
  with two restrictions that need no new analysis:

  - Leading guards only — a guard-exit either exits or falls through unchanged,
  so until some other statement runs, the operands still hold their loop-top
  values. The run stops at the first non-guard statement (which might reassign
  them).
  - Exact complement of the loop condition only — <↔>=, <=↔>, etc., same
  variable, same bound (name / literal / .length extent). The loop re-checks its
  condition every iteration, so that's invariant regardless of how the body
  mutates things.

  So it catches "you already checked that in the loop header" and nothing it
  can't be sure of.

  Verified

  - Positive flags; router.arche/stdlib/core produce zero false positives (the
  unsound version's hits are gone).
  - Soundness edges: guard on a different var/bound, guard after a reassignment,
  decrement loops → all correctly not flagged.
  - -Werror clean, 591 tests, ASan 6/6, C units pass, arche explain W0020 works.
  - New: tests/.../policy/redundant_guard.arche (positive +
  same-loop-different-bound negative, count==1), docs/explain/W0020.md.
  Default-on lint, @allow(redundant_guard) escape hatch.

  One test-authoring gotcha worth noting: the build's success line prints the
  output path, which contained the substring redundant_guard (the test filename)
  — so the count grep had to match the bracketed [redundant_guard] tag, not the
  bare slug.

      55  ## Why Arche?
      56  
      57  Arche is a playground for **data-oriented design (DOD)** as a l
          anguage, not a pattern you
     ...
      62  - **Data-oriented by default** - think in columns and whole-col
          lection transforms, not objects and element loops.
      63  - **Database-style data model** - archetypes are tables defined
           by a set of component types; a system is a query that runs ove
          r every matching table.
      64  - **No implicit heap** - all storage is static and planned upfr
          ont, so memory behavior is fully predictable. That's a property
           of the *language core*, **not** a ceiling on the data model: "
          no dynamic allocation" does **not** mean "no dynamic archetypes
          ". **Dynamic (resizable) archetypes — the backbone of a full EC
          S — are on the roadmap as a library** built on the same columna
          r model, once the core language matures; the core just doesn't 
          bake in implicit allocation.
      29 -- **Libraries as devices & drivers** - a *device* is a library 
         -that declares shapes + systems but owns no storage; the *driver
         -* (your program) picks the pool sizes and runs the device's sys
         -tems. A hardware metaphor for dependency injection: the storage
         - owner is always the caller, never the library.                
      65 +- **Libraries as devices & drivers** - a *device* is a library 
         +that declares shapes + systems but owns no storage; the *driver
         +* (your program) picks the pool sizes and runs the device's sys
         +tems. A hardware metaphor for dependency injection: the storage
         + owner is always the caller, never the library. See [docs/devic
         +es.md](docs/devices.md).                                       
      66  - **The "function," split four ways** - most languages overload
           one `function` keyword for jobs that have nothing in common. A
          rche gives each job its own form, and the grammar enforces the 
          split (see below).
      67  - **Crashes are opt-in and visible** - the rare op that can sti
          ll fail at runtime (an out-of-bounds index, a full pool) carrie
          s a *failure policy* right at the site: `a[i] !clamp`, `n / d !
          zero`, `a[i] !undefined`. A `func` defaults to `clamp`, so an u
          nannotated op in one can't crash; a `proc` defaults to `!abort`
          , the deliberate crash site. `--no-abort` bans every abort site
           (implicit or explicit) — but that is **not** a whole-build cra
          sh-free *proof*: `!undefined` is a raw unchecked op (UB can sti
          ll fault), and a custom `policy` is your own code (it can call 
          `_exit` or leave an op out of bounds). `--no-undefined` also ba
          ns the unsafe opt-out; the rest is on the policies you write.
      68  
     ...
      80  | `policy` | `name :: policy(len, i)`    | no          | a fail
          ure macro — inlined at a fallible op (`a[i] !clamp`) to resolve
           the failure *at the site* |

           step;

           xs[k] !clamp

  Wrote 2 memories (ctrl+o to expand)

● Done. Yes — and shipped a focused version of it.

  What changed

  E0213 policy_uses_undefined — a policy body may no longer use !undefined (the
  raw, unchecked op that can read out of bounds / divide by zero). A policy is
  the safety mechanism — it's inlined at every fallible op that selects it — so
  a raw op inside one silently breaks safety everywhere it's used. Enforced in
  the shared validate_explicit_policy, so it covers bounds and divide
  !undefined, independent of --no-undefined.

  EVIL:  z := scratch[i] !undefined;   // inside a policy → E0213

  Why this exact rule (not "must be provably in bounds")

  I investigated first, and the literal "ban unprovable indexing" would have
  broken shipped, legitimate code: pool overflow handler policies (@policy(pool)
  … ?evict) scan a column to pick an eviction victim — Foo.ts[j] bounded by the
  scalar count param, which the prover can't statically tie to the pool. Those
  are safe because they clamp (total), just not provable.

  So the guarantee is "a policy can never perform a raw/UB op" — every fallible
  op in a policy is either provably safe or resolves to a total policy (default
  clamp/zero). Unprovable-but-clamped stays allowed; only the raw opt-out is
  banned. Combined with the pre-existing E0098 (!abort forbidden in a policy), a
  policy can't OOB and can't crash — except deliberate _exit for
  abort-category, which is termination, not UB.

  What I deliberately did not restrict

  - Effects — the canonical abort policy calls dprintf (stderr) + _exit
  (terminate), both foreign. Banning effects would break it, and side effects
  aren't unsafe (no UB). So policies may still have effects.
  - One sharp edge remains: a user-defined empty policy (@policy(bounds) raw :: 
  policy(len,i){}) is !undefined by another name; using it inside another policy
  is a deliberate raw opt-out. Catching it needs policy-totality analysis
  (undecidable in general). Documented in explain/E0213.md.

  Verified

  - -Werror clean, 592 tests (new: policy_uses_undefined_rejected.arche), ASan
  6/6, arche explain E0213 works.
  - EVIL policy (!undefined, bounds + divide) → rejected; legit eviction
  handlers and all core/stdlib policies → unaffected.

  Want me to also close the empty-policy edge (flag any policy applied inside a
  policy whose body doesn't mutate its operands — a cheap, conservative totality
  proxy)? It's contrived but I can make the guarantee airtight if you want.

     → SIGSEGV (stack overflow from infinite policy inlining)

● Confirmed: SIGSEGV (exit 139) — stack overflow from infinite macro inlining.

  Answer

  Yes — and it already happens implicitly. A policy is a macro inlined at a
  fallible op, and every fallible op in a policy body itself carries a policy
  (the func default, clamp). So policy-in-policy is the norm, not the exception:
  - The eviction handler's Foo.ts[j] clamps — that's the clamp policy inlined
  inside the eviction policy.
  - Explicit nesting works too: buf[i] !clamp inside another policy compiles and
  runs.

  That part is fine and intended.

  The problem your question found

  There's no recursion guard, so a policy that applies itself — directly or in a
  cycle — to a checked index inlines forever and crashes the compiler:

  @policy(bounds) evil :: policy(len: int, i: int) {
    buf: int[3];
    i = buf[i] !evil;   // → SIGSEGV (stack overflow), just reproduced
  }

  (My earlier cycle tests looked fine only because they indexed a module-static
  array, which codegen exempts from the policy path — so the nesting never
  re-entered. A local/param-array index does re-enter, and dies.)

  This is a real bug — a compiler crash on input that passes semantic analysis.

  Fix I'd propose

  Detect policy cycles at semantic time → clean error, no crash. Build a graph
  of policy → policies it explicitly applies (!P in its body), DFS for a cycle,
  emit a new E0214 cyclic_policy ("policy evil applies itself — policies may not
  recurse").
  - Doesn't restrict legit acyclic nesting (!clamp inside a handler stays fine).
  - The implicit-default path can't cycle (defaults resolve to core total
  policies like clamp/zero, which don't index), so only explicit !P references
  need graphing.

  Cleaner than a codegen depth-cap (which would error late and arbitrarily).
  Want me to implement the cycle detection?

      4311 +/* The policy a fallible op explicitly applies (`expr !P`), r
           +esolved by the op's category — or NULL.                      
      4312 + * Bounds for index/slice, divide for `/`,`%`; `?`-handlers (
           +pool) are a separate mechanism. */                           
      4313 +static DeclSummary *policy_op_applies(SemanticContext *ctx, S
           +yntaxView v) {                                               
      4314 +  PolicyCategory cat;                                        
      4315 +  SyntaxNodeKind k = sv_kind(v);                             
      4316 +  if (k == SN_INDEX_EXPR || k == SN_SLICE_EXPR) {            
      4317 +    cat = POLICY_CAT_BOUNDS;                                 
      4318 +  } else if (k == SN_BINARY_EXPR) {                          
      4319 +    int op = sem_binary_op(v);                               
      4320 +    if (op != OP_DIV && op != OP_MOD)                        
      4321 +      return NULL;                                           
      4322 +    cat = POLICY_CAT_DIVIDE;                                 
      4323 +  } else {                                                   
      4324 +    return NULL;                                             
      4325 +  }                                                          
      4326 +  SyntaxView pol = sv_child(v, SN_POLICY_REF);               
      4327 +  if (!sv_present(pol) || sv_token(pol, TOK_QUESTION).ptr) /*
           + `!` panic policies only */                                  
      4328 +    return NULL;                                             
      4329 +  SynText t = sv_token(pol, TOK_IDENT);                      
      4330 +  if (!t.ptr)                                                
      4331 +    return NULL;                                             
      4332 +  char *nm = sem_txt_dup(t);                                 
      4333 +  DeclSummary *p = nm ? find_policy_sig_cat(ctx, nm, cat) : N
           +ULL;                                                         
      4334 +  free(nm);                                                  
      4335 +  return p;                                                  
      4336 +}                                                            
      4337 +                                                             
      4338 +/* Collect (deduped, capped) every policy the subtree `v` exp
           +licitly applies. */                                          
      4339 +static void policy_edges_walk(SemanticContext *ctx, SyntaxVie
           +w v, DeclSummary **out, int *n, int cap) {                   
      4340 +  if (!sv_present(v))                                        
      4341 +    return;                                                  
      4342 +  DeclSummary *p = policy_op_applies(ctx, v);                
      4343 +  if (p) {                                                   
      4344 +    int dup = 0;                                             
      4345 +    for (int i = 0; i < *n; i++)                             
      4346 +      if (out[i] == p) {                                     
      4347 +        dup = 1;                                             
      4348 +        break;                                               
      4349 +      }                                                      
      4350 +    if (!dup && *n < cap)                                    
      4351 +      out[(*n)++] = p;                                       
      4352 +  }                                                          
      4353 +  for (int i = 0; i < v.node->child_count; i++)              
      4354 +    if (v.node->children[i].tag == SE_NODE)                  
      4355 +      policy_edges_walk(ctx, (SyntaxView){v.node->children[i]
           +.as.node, v.src}, out, n, cap);                              
      4356 +}                                                            
      4357 +                                                             
      4358 +/* True if policy `cur` can reach `target` through explicit `
           +!P` applications. `seen` prevents infinite                   
      4359 + * looping in THIS traversal (we're detecting cycles), bounde
           +d by `cap`. */                                               
      4360 +static int policy_reaches(SemanticContext *ctx, DeclSummary *
           +cur, DeclSummary *target, DeclSummary **seen,                
      4361 +                          int *seen_n, int cap) {            
      4362 +  if (!cur || !sv_present(cur->body_node))                   
      4363 +    return 0;                                                
      4364 +  DeclSummary *edges[32];                                    
      4365 +  int en = 0;                                                
      4366 +  for (int i = 0, m = sem_stmt_count(cur->body_node); i < m; 
           +i++)                                                         
      4367 +    policy_edges_walk(ctx, sem_stmt_at(cur->body_node, i), ed
           +ges, &en, 32);                                               
      4368 +  for (int i = 0; i < en; i++) {                             
      4369 +    if (edges[i] == target)                                  
      4370 +      return 1;                                              
      4371 +    int visited = 0;                                         
      4372 +    for (int j = 0; j < *seen_n; j++)                        
      4373 +      if (seen[j] == edges[i]) {                             
      4374 +        visited = 1;                                         
      4375 +        break;                                               
      4376 +      }                                                      
      4377 +    if (!visited && *seen_n < cap) {                         
      4378 +      seen[(*seen_n)++] = edges[i];                          
      4379 +      if (policy_reaches(ctx, edges[i], target, seen, seen_n,
           + cap))                                                       
      4380 +        return 1;                                            
      4381 +    }                                                        
      4382 +  }                                                          
      4383 +  return 0;                                                  
      4384 +}                                                            
      4385 +                                                             
      4386 +/* E0214: a policy is inlined as a MACRO, so a policy that ap
           +plies itself (directly or transitively)                      
      4387 + * would expand forever — a codegen stack overflow. Detect su
           +ch cycles over explicit `!P` applications                    
      4388 + * in policy bodies and reject before codegen. (The implicit 
           +default of an unannotated op resolves to a                   
      4389 + * core total policy that doesn't index, so it can't close a 
           +cycle — only explicit `!P` can.) */                          
      4390 +static void sem_check_policy_cycles(SemanticContext *ctx) {  
      4391 +  for (int di = 0; di < ctx->decl_count; di++) {             
      4392 +    DeclSummary *d = ctx->decls[di];                         
      4393 +    if (!d->is_policy || d->is_extern || !sv_present(d->body_
           +node))                                                       
      4394 +      continue;                                              
      4395 +    DeclSummary *seen[64];                                   
      4396 +    int seen_n = 0;                                          
      4397 +    if (policy_reaches(ctx, d, d, seen, &seen_n, 64))        
      4398 +      sem_emit_cyclic_policy(ctx, d->loc, d->name ? d->name :
           + "<anon>");                                                  
      4399 +  }                                                          
      4400 +}                                                            
      4401 +                                                             
      4402  /* Failure-policy validation: walk every proc/func/policy bod
            y and check each index/slice op's policy
      4403   * against the prover's verdict (proven-safe ⇒ no policy; pro
            vably-OOB ⇒ error; unprovable ⇒ validate
      4404   * the explicit `!name`). See bnd_policy_check for the emitte
            d diagnostics (E0097-99/E0124/W0018). */

● Done. The compiler crash is fixed.

  What changed

  E0214 cyclic_policy — a policy that applies itself (directly or transitively
  via explicit !P) is now rejected at semantic time with a clean error, instead
  of SIGSEGV-ing the compiler at codegen.

  @policy(bounds) evil :: policy(len: int, i: int) {
    buf: int[3];
    i = buf[i] !evil;   // before: SIGSEGV (infinite inlining) — now: E0214
  }

  How

  sem_check_policy_cycles (runs right after sem_check_policies):
  - policy_op_applies resolves each fallible op's explicit !P to a policy decl
  by category (bounds for index/slice, divide for //%; ! only, not ?-handlers).
  - Builds the policy→policy "applies" graph by walking each policy body, DFS
  for reachability P → … → P, emits E0214 on each policy in a cycle.
  - The error stops the build before codegen, so the stack overflow never
  happens.

  Correctly scoped

  - Self-reference and mutual cycles (pa↔pb) → both caught. Verified.
  - Acyclic nesting stays fine — table[i] !clamp inside another policy
  compiles+runs (ok). Only cycles are rejected.
  - The implicit default can't cycle — an unannotated op in a policy resolves to
  a core total policy (clamp/zero) that doesn't index, so only explicit !P
  references are graphed. This is why the normal, implicit policy-in-policy
  (e.g. the eviction handler's clamping Foo.ts[j]) is untouched.

  Verified

  - Self-ref (was exit 139 / SIGSEGV) → clean E0214; mutual cycle → E0214 on
  both.
  - -Werror clean, 593 tests (new cyclic_policy_rejected.arche), ASan 6/6, arche
  explain E0214 works.

  So, to close the loop on your original question: yes, policy-in-policy is 
  allowed and in fact happens implicitly everywhere — but it must be acyclic,
  and the compiler now enforces that instead of dying on a cycle.

● Update(README.md)
  ⎿  Added 1 line, removed 1 line
      64  - **No implicit heap** - all storage is static and planned upfr
          ont, so memory behavior is fully predictable. That's a property
           of the *language core*, **not** a ceiling on the data model: "
          no dynamic allocation" does **not** mean "no dynamic archetypes
          ". **Dynamic (resizable) archetypes — the backbone of a full EC
          S — are on the roadmap as a library** built on the same columna
          r model, once the core language matures; the core just doesn't 
          bake in implicit allocation.
      65  - **Libraries as devices & drivers** - a *device* is a library 
          that declares shapes + systems but owns no storage; the *driver
          * (your program) picks the pool sizes and runs the device's sys
          tems. A hardware metaphor for dependency injection: the storage
           owner is always the caller, never the library. See [docs/devic
          es.md](docs/devices.md).
      66  - **The "function," split four ways** - most languages overload
           one `function` keyword for jobs that have nothing in common. A
          rche gives each job its own form, and the grammar enforces the 
          split (see below).
      67 -- **Crashes are opt-in and visible** - the rare op that can sti
         -ll fail at runtime (an out-of-bounds index, a full pool) carrie
         -s a *failure policy* right at the site: `a[i] !clamp`, `n / d !
         -zero`, `a[i] !undefined`. A `func` defaults to `clamp`, so an u
         -nannotated op in one can't crash; a `proc` defaults to `!abort`
         -, the deliberate crash site. `--no-abort` bans every abort site
         - (implicit or explicit) — but that is **not** a whole-build cra
         -sh-free *proof*: `!undefined` is a raw unchecked op (UB can sti
         -ll fault), and a custom `policy` is your own code (it can call 
         -`_exit` or leave an op out of bounds). `--no-undefined` also ba
         -ns the unsafe opt-out; the rest is on the policies you write.  
      67 +- **Crashes are opt-in and visible** - the rare op that can sti
         +ll fail at runtime (an out-of-bounds index, a full pool) carrie
         +s a *failure policy* right at the site: `a[i] !clamp`, `n / d !
         +zero`, `a[i] !undefined`. A `func` defaults to `clamp`, so an u
         +nannotated op in one can't crash; a `proc` defaults to `!abort`

● Update memory:

  Recalled 1 memory, wrote 4 memories (ctrl+o to expand)

● Done. !undefined is now opt-in instead of opt-out.

  The flip

  - Default: !undefined (the raw, runtime-unsafe op — UB if out of range) is
  forbidden in user code → E0126. An ordinary build/run/check rejects it.
  - Opt in: --allow-undefined permits it (and --unchecked implies it, since it
  strips checks to undefined anyway).
  - The old opt-out flag --no-undefined is removed (redundant — that's now the
  default).

  What changed

  - semantic: g_no_undefined → g_allow_undefined (default 0); the check became
  !g_allow_undefined && decl_is_user_code(d). core/stdlib stay exempt.
  - CLI: --allow-undefined added to build, run, and check (a default restriction
  needs its escape hatch wherever it applies — run/check only had build's
  safety flags absent before).
  - E0126 message rewritten: "forbidden by default … use a checked total policy,
  make it provable, or pass --allow-undefined."
  - This composes with the prior policy work: !undefined is never allowed inside
  a policy body (E0213), regardless of this flag.

  Tests + docs

  - 4 tests that legitimately exercise the raw op (undefined_raw, pool_column,
  pool_evict, pool_override) got --allow-undefined on their RUN lines.
  - enforce/no_undefined_rejects.arche → repurposed to
  undefined_forbidden_by_default.arche (asserts both: rejected by default,
  permitted with the flag).
  - New docs/explain/E0126.md; updated README (2 spots) + docs/language.md (3
  spots) to the opt-in model.

  Verified

  - -Werror build clean; 593 tests; ASan 6/6.
  - check default → "forbidden by default"; check --allow-undefined → permits.
  --help lists the flag.
  - README/taste examples unaffected (no !undefined).

  Net: the safe thing is the default everywhere, and you now deliberately opt
  into the unsafe op rather than remembering to opt out.

● Update(arche_analyzer.c)
  ⎿  Added 28 lines
      493    emit_syn(anchor.line, anchor.column, 1, 1, "type", display_t
           ype(ty));
      494  }
      495  
      496 +/* The same inferred-type hint, for a top-level value/storage 
          +DECLARATION (`MAX :: 100`, `count := 0`).                     
      497 + * A decl's name is a leading token (not an expr child like a 
          +statement bind), so the value is the                          
      498 + * decl's first expression. This keeps `::` / `:=` consistent 
          +at every scope — file scope included.                         
      499 + * Skips type aliases and any decl that already states its typ
          +e. */                                                         
      500 +static void emit_decl_type_hint(SyntaxView decl, SemanticConte
          +xt *ctx) {                                                    
      501 +  const SemModel *model = sem_context_model(ctx);             
      502 +  if (sem_model_bind_alias(model, sv_id(decl)))               
      503 +    return; /* a nominal type alias, not a value */           
      504 +  if (sv_type_count(decl) > 0)                                
      505 +    return; /* explicit declared type already in the source */
      506 +  SyntaxView value = sv_expr_at(decl, 0); /* with no written t
          +ype, the first expr IS the value */                           
      507 +  if (!sv_present(value))                                     
      508 +    return; /* e.g. a zero-init buffer `buf : T` (no value) */
      509 +  TypeId tid = sem_model_expr_type_id(model, sv_id(value));   
      510 +  if (tid == TYID_UNKNOWN)                                    
      511 +    return;                                                   
      512 +  char tybuf[128];                                            
      513 +  const char *ty = tyid_display(sem_context_arena(ctx), tid, t
          +ybuf, sizeof(tybuf));                                         
      514 +  if (!ty || !ty[0])                                          
      515 +    return;                                                   
      516 +  CvPos anchor = sv_token_pos(decl, TOK_EQ); /* the `=` of `:=
          +`, else the 2nd `:` of `::` */                                
      517 +  if (!anchor.line)                                           
      518 +    anchor = sv_token_pos_at(decl, TOK_COLON, 1);             
      519 +  if (!anchor.line)                                           
      520 +    return;                                                   
      521 +  emit_syn(anchor.line, anchor.column, 1, 1, "type", display_t
          +ype(ty));                                                     
      522 +}                                                             
      523 +                                                              
      524  /* A type position naming an alias shows its backing type, e.g
           . `count (int)`. */
      525  static void emit_typeref_hint(SyntaxView tr, SemanticContext *
           ctx) {
      526    SynText id = sv_token(tr, TOK_IDENT);

❯ if it doesn't have a type then its a hack. Fundementally that is the grammar. 

● Right — that reframes it cleanly and correctly. The unified grammar is name : 
  ⟨type⟩ : value; the inlay's whole job is to reconstruct the elided ⟨type⟩ slot
  = the type of the RHS. Every RHS is a form with a type, so "no type" is never
  a legitimate outcome — it means the compiler isn't typing that form yet,
  which is the hack to fix. Let me finalize the plan around that.

● Updated plan

● The plan now reflects your principle: the inlay is a pure projection of
  type-of(RHS), every RHS is a typed form per the grammar, and where the type
  system doesn't yet produce a type for a form (func/proc/sys/arche exprs)
  that's the gap to close — not a binding to skip. The analyzer ends up with one
  rule and zero kind logic.

● User approved Claude's plan
  ⎿  Plan saved to: ~/.claude/plans/1-lets-plan-this-parsed-wigderson.md · /plan
     to edit
     Plan: the type inlay = the elided ⟨type⟩ slot, for every binding

     Context

     The unified grammar is name : ⟨type⟩ (: | =) value — x := 5 is x : 
     ⟨inferred⟩ = 5, MAX :: 100
     is MAX : ⟨inferred⟩ : 100. The inlay's entire job is to fill the elided 
     ⟨type⟩ slot so the
     view reads as the longhand. So the rule is singular and grammar-derived:
     show type-of(RHS) whenever
     the ⟨type⟩ slot is elided — for every binding form. There is no "this one
     has no type": in the
     grammar every RHS is a form with a type. If the compiler can't produce one,
     that's the hack to fix, not
     a binding to skip.

     Today it's inconsistent: locals (:=/:: in a body) get the hint; file-scope
     MAX :: 100,
     count := 0, add :: func(...), step :: sys(...), Particle :: arche {...} get
     nothing.

     Root cause (verified). sem_model_set_expr_type_id (the per-node type the
     LSP reads) has one
     caller: analyze_expression (semantic.c:2046-2056) — the single home for an
     expression's type. Locals
     work because their RHS flows through it. Two gaps make the rest
     inconsistent:
     1. Top-level const/static initializers are never routed through
     analyze_expression (the const pass
     uses the lighter resolve_expression_type, no model write).
     2. analyze_expression's switch has no case for 
     SN_FUNC_EXPR/SN_PROC_EXPR/SN_SYS_EXPR/
     SN_ARCH_EXPR — those RHS forms are never assigned a type at all. That's the
     "hack": a form the
     grammar says has a type, that the type system doesn't produce.

     My earlier per-kind backfills (analyzer emit_decl_type_hint, a static
     recording, a const post-pass)
     are exactly the picking-and-choosing to avoid. Revert them.

     Approach — type every RHS form; one uniform key; one analyzer rule

     1. Type every RHS form (close the hack), in the one home. Extend the type
     system so every binding's
     RHS has an interned TypeId, recorded in the model:
     - SN_FUNC_EXPR / SN_PROC_EXPR → the structural TYK_FUNC id (assemble from
     param+return ids /
     reuse the contract type already interned for structural checks).
     - SN_ARCH_EXPR → TYK_ARCHETYPE_CATEGORY (exists).
     - SN_SYS_EXPR → its callable-over-components type; if the arena has no
     representation yet, add a
     minimal one (a TYK_FUNC-style or a dedicated kind) so tyid_display can
     render it. This is the
     genuine type-system gap the user is pointing at — fill it, don't skip.
     - meters :: float (RHS is a type) → the type meta-type. (Keep the existing
     alias-backing inlay if
     preferred; decide during impl — but it is a type, not "nothing".)
        Add these as cases where expression typing happens (analyze_expression,
     semantic.c:1726), so the
        model is written via the existing single home.

     2. Route every binding's RHS through that home, keyed uniformly by the 
     binding/decl node.
     - Locals (SN_BIND_STMT): already analyzed; additionally record the type by
     the bind node id so
     all bindings share one key (one line after the RHS is analyzed).
     - Top-level value bindings (SN_CONST_DECL value consts, SN_STATIC_DECL):
     run the RHS through
     analyze_expression after the const fixpoint (so LIMIT :: MAX resolves),
     recording by the decl
     node id. Skip type aliases only because their inlay is the backing-type
     form, not because they lack
     a type. Verify no duplicated diagnostics (RHS already resolved); fallback =
     record the
     already-interned type directly if re-analysis double-emits.
     - Func/proc/sys/arche decls (SN_*_DECL): record the form's interned type
     (from step 1) by the decl
     node id.

     3. Analyzer — ONE rule, zero kind logic. Replace emit_bind_hint + my
     emit_decl_type_hint with a
        single emit_type_hint(node, ctx): if the ⟨type⟩ slot is written
     (sv_type_count(node) > 0),
        return; read tid = sem_model_expr_type_id(model, sv_id(node)); emit it
     at the anchor (TOK_EQ for
        :=, else 2nd TOK_COLON for ::) via display_type(tyid_display(...)). Wire
     it for every
        binding/decl node kind in walk. A node shows a hint iff the compiler
     recorded its RHS type and the
        source didn't already state it — no node is special-cased.

     Why this matches the principle

     The inlay is a pure projection of type-of(RHS). Every RHS is a typed form
     (the grammar guarantees
     it); the implementation's job is only to make the type system actually
     produce that type for each form
     (step 1) and surface it (steps 2-3). Removing a kind later = nothing in the
     analyzer; the type system
     still has the type, we just wouldn't render it.

     Files

     - semantic/sem_types.{c,h} — a sys/meta type representation + tyid_display
     cases, if missing.
     - semantic/semantic.c — type the SN_*_EXPR forms in analyze_expression;
     route const/static RHS +
     decl forms into the model by node id; one line in SN_BIND_STMT; revert this
     session's scattered
     recordings.
     - arche_analyzer.c — one emit_type_hint; wire decl node kinds in walk.
     - Reuse: sem_model_set_expr_type_id, value_const_type, sem_tyid_of_name,
     tyid_display,
     tyid_of_func/contract builder, sv_token_pos/_at, display_type.

     Verification

     - make (-Werror), make test (was 593), make test-asan; no duplicated 
     diagnostics.
     - arche-analyzer --dump on a file with every form — a type hint on EACH
     elided binding: MAX→i32,
     ratio→float, LIMIT :: MAX→i32, count := 0→i32, add :: func→func type, step 
     :: sys→sys type,
     Particle :: arche→archetype, every local; and none where ⟨type⟩ is already
     written
     (total : int = 5, buf : int[8]). The success criterion is the absence of
     any special-casing — if
     it parses as a binding, it shows its RHS type.
     - Existing explicit_view/* tests pass; add bind_type_hints.arche covering
     all forms in one file.

❯ what is func.is_proc?? they are different things. Why would there ever be a   
  flag like that?                                                               

● Fair hit. func.is_proc is a pre-existing discriminant in the type arena (I
  didn't add it — tyid_of_func(..., is_proc) and TYK_FUNC{...,is_proc} were
  already there). What it does: proc-types and func-types share the same
  param/return structure, so they're stored as one TYK_FUNC node with is_proc
  telling them apart — and it does keep them distinct (a proc(int)→… and
  func(int)→… never unify).

  But you're right that it's wrong-headed for Arche specifically: a proc is not 
  a kind of func — the split is the bedrock of the language, so naming the
  shared kind TYK_FUNC and flagging procs into it conflates the two exact things
  the grammar keeps apart. And it bit me immediately: I shoved sys in as
  is_proc=1 too (a sys isn't a proc either — that's why it printed
  proc(<unknown>, …)). Let me check how entangled the flag is before fixing it
  properly.

● The plan now incorporates both of your points: distinct
  TYK_FUNC/TYK_PROC/TYK_SYS kinds (the is_proc flag deleted, structural
  inequality falling out of the kind), and hide-redundant-by-default with opt-in
